        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771"><meta property="og:title" content="3D Sodiophilic Ti3C2 MXene@g-C3N4 Hetero-Interphase Raises the Stability of Sodium Metal Anodes"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/acsnano.2c07771.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Owing to several advantages of metallic sodium (Na), such as a relatively high theoretical capacity, low redox potential, wide availability, and low cost, Na metal batteries are being extensively studied, which are expected to play a major role in the fields of electric vehicles and grid-scale energy storage. Although considerable efforts have been devoted to utilizing MXene-based materials for suppressing Na dendrites, achieving a stable cycling of Na metal anodes remains extremely challenging due to, for example, the low Coulombic efficiency (CE) caused by the severe side reactions. Herein, a g-C3N4 layer was attached in situ on the Ti3C2 MXene surface, inducing a surface state reconstruction and thus forming a stable hetero-interphase with excellent sodiophilicity between the MXene and g-C3N4 to inhibit side reactions and guide uniform Na ion flux. The 3D construction can not only lower the local current density to facilitate uniform Na plating/stripping but also mitigate volume change to stabilize the electrolyte/electrode interphase. Thus, the 3D Ti3C2 MXene@g-C3N4 nanocomposite enables much enhanced average CEs (99.9% at 1 mA h cm–2, 0.5 mA cm–2) in asymmetric half cells, long-term stability (up to 700 h) for symmetric cells, and stable cycling (up to 800 cycles at 2 C), together with outstanding rate capability (up to 20 C), of full cells. The present study demonstrates an approach in developing practically high performance for Na metal anodes."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/acsnano.2c07771.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6ed8dab29775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ed8dab29775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '28f5e7c7-ab7d-4a25-88c8-752698d9e156'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6ed8dab29775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

$(function() {
    var serialCode = "ancac3";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D Sodiophilic Ti&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;C&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; MXene@g-C&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;N&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Hetero-Interphase Raises the Stability of Sodium Metal Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c07771", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D Sodiophilic Ti&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;C&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; MXene@g-C&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;N&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Hetero-Interphase Raises the Stability of Sodium Metal Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c07771", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D Sodiophilic Ti&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;C&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; MXene@g-C&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;N&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Hetero-Interphase Raises the Stability of Sodium Metal Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c07771", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D Sodiophilic Ti&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;C&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; MXene@g-C&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;N&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Hetero-Interphase Raises the Stability of Sodium Metal Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c07771", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D Sodiophilic Ti&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;C&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; MXene@g-C&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;N&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Hetero-Interphase Raises the Stability of Sodium Metal Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c07771", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D Sodiophilic Ti&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;C&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; MXene@g-C&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;N&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Hetero-Interphase Raises the Stability of Sodium Metal Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c07771", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D Sodiophilic Ti&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;C&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; MXene@g-C&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;N&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Hetero-Interphase Raises the Stability of Sodium Metal Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c07771", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D Sodiophilic Ti&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;C&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; MXene@g-C&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;N&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Hetero-Interphase Raises the Stability of Sodium Metal Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c07771", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "3D Sodiophilic Ti&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;C&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt; MXene@g-C&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;N&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt; Hetero-Interphase Raises the Stability of Sodium Metal Anodes", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsnano.2c07771", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

and Engineering, Tongji University, Shanghai201804, China</div></div><div class="loa-info-scifinder"><span>More by <a href="/action/doSearch?field1=Contrib&amp;text1=Zhenghui++Pan" class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D17197%26pageCount%3D13%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DChangyuan%2BBao%252C%2BJunhui%2BWang%252C%2BBo%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D10%26contentID%3Dacsnano.2c07771%26title%3D3D%2BSodiophilic%2BTi3C2%2BMXene%2540g-C3N4%2BHetero-Interphase%2BRaises%2Bthe%2BStability%2Bof%2BSodium%2BMetal%2BAnodes%26numPages%3D13%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D17209%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2022" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/medium/nn2c07771_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c07771&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Owing to several advantages of metallic sodium (Na), such as a relatively high theoretical capacity, low redox potential, wide availability, and low cost, Na metal batteries are being extensively studied, which are expected to play a major role in the fields of electric vehicles and grid-scale energy storage. Although considerable efforts have been devoted to utilizing MXene-based materials for suppressing Na dendrites, achieving a stable cycling of Na metal anodes remains extremely challenging due to, for example, the low Coulombic efficiency (CE) caused by the severe side reactions. Herein, a g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer was attached <i>in situ</i> on the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene surface, inducing a surface state reconstruction and thus forming a stable hetero-interphase with excellent sodiophilicity between the MXene and g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> to inhibit side reactions and guide uniform Na ion flux. The 3D construction can not only lower the local current density to facilitate uniform Na plating/stripping but also mitigate volume change to stabilize the electrolyte/electrode interphase. Thus, the 3D Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite enables much enhanced average CEs (99.9% at 1 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>, 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>) in asymmetric half cells, long-term stability (up to 700 h) for symmetric cells, and stable cycling (up to 800 cycles at 2 C), together with outstanding rate capability (up to 20 C), of full cells. The present study demonstrates an approach in developing practically high performance for Na metal anodes.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D17197%26pageCount%3D13%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DChangyuan%2BBao%252C%2BJunhui%2BWang%252C%2BBo%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D10%26contentID%3Dacsnano.2c07771%26title%3D3D%2BSodiophilic%2BTi3C2%2BMXene%2540g-C3N4%2BHetero-Interphase%2BRaises%2Bthe%2BStability%2Bof%2BSodium%2BMetal%2BAnodes%26numPages%3D13%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1936-0851%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dancac3%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D17209%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2022" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2022 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290743&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Deposition" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Deposition" class="article__tags__link">Deposition</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrochemical%20cells" class="article__tags__link">Electrochemical cells</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290797&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Metals" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Metals" class="article__tags__link">Metals</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ti3c2+Mxene%40g-c3n4&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Ti3C2%20MXene%40g-C3N4">Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodiophilicity&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodiophilicity">sodiophilicity</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Dendrite-free&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_dendrite-free">dendrite-free</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na+Metal+Anodes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na%20metal%20anodes">Na metal anodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Na+Metal+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Na%20metal%20batteries">Na metal batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">Together with the rapid development of new mobility, such as portable electronic devices, electric-powered vehicles, and the Internet of Things, advanced energy storage technologies beyond lithium (Li)-ion batteries are tremendously desirable for a wide range of applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> Although Na-ion batteries possess many advantages including high abundance of Na resources, low cost, and analogous battery chemistry to that of Li-ion batteries, they still cannot meet the growing demands for energy storage of high energy density and long cycling, because of the insufficient capacities of anode materials and poor cycling ability. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 6 to 8">(6−8)</a> Employing a Na metal anode is the most promising approach by virtue of its high theoretical capacity (1166 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) and low redox potential (−2.71 V vs standard hydrogen electrode). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> Similar to Li metal anodes, the practical application of Na metal anodes is largely hindered by multifaceted unresolved severe issues and challenges, e.g., Na dendrite growth, low Coulombic efficiency, and large volume change. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> First, nonuniform Na<sup>+</sup> flux can steadily lead to uncontrolled dendrite growth. During the repeated plating/stripping process, Na dendrites will continue to grow and eventually pierce the membrane, causing severe safety concerns due to the internal short circuits. Another big problem is that Na metal has a very high reactivity in conventional electrolytes, such that an unstable solid electrolyte interface (SEI) is formed once the Na metal is in contact with electrolytes. However, the <i>in situ</i> generated SEI layer is unable to keep electrolytes from eroding the Na metal or withstanding Na dendrite growth and therefore constantly ruptures and rebuilds during the Na plating and stripping process, resulting in rapid capacity decay and low Coulombic efficiency (CE). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> A Na metal anode is also continuously enlarged during the cycling process, especially at high capacity and high current density, and therefore the corrosion and pulverization of the electrode are likely to occur, which would cause the battery to fail. Indeed, these issues are not independent of each other but are closely intertwined.</div><div class="NLM_p">On the other hand, although the development of Na metal batteries is still at its initial stage, considerable progress has been made with Na metal anodes, where there have been great efforts to revive safe and high-energy-density Na metal batteries (NMBs), such as Na–O<sub>2</sub>, Na–S, and Na–Se batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 13 to 16">(13−16)</a> In terms of electrolytes, several approaches, such as the use of electrolyte additives, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> high-concentrated electrolytes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> ionic liquids, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> and the development of solid-state electrolytes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> have been taken to improve the stability of Na metal anodes. One among the other strategies is to construct a stable interphase between the Na metal anode and the electrolyte prior to cell assembly. To date, there have been several types of artificial films reported for Na metal anodes, e.g., NaBr coating, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> NaF coating, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> ALD Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> coating, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> multilayer graphene films, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> a protective polyvinylidene difluoride (PVDF) layer, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> and amorphous Na<sub>3</sub>PS<sub>4</sub> layers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> In terms of the electrode structure, a low local current density helps to inhibit dendrite growth caused by concentration polarization, and therefore three-dimensional structures with a high specific surface area have been widely designed. In addition, the volume change of Na is also an unsolved serious problem, which can be effectively alleviated by introducing metallic Na into the gaps of nanostructured host materials. Recently, several types of substrates/hosts with nano/microstructural designs (e.g., porous Al foil, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> 3D Cu with nanowires, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> porous 3D Ni, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> macroporous catalytic carbon nanotemplates, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> reduced graphene oxide, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> carbonized wood <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a>) have been constructed in attempts to achieve stable Na metal anodes. Among the various matrixes, carbon-based 3D materials are regarded as the most popular hosts due to their ease of fabrication, low cost, and stable chemical properties. For example, a reduced graphene oxide aerogel (rGa) was synthesized to host a metallic Na anode by Chen’s group, via an oriented freeze-drying method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> After melt infusion of Na, the Na@rGa with a low overpotential (50 mV) delivered a stable cycling performance for 1000 cycles at 5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>. Quan’s group reported a 3D-printed hierarchical graphene lattice that was shown to exhibit rather stable Na plating/stripping behavior with a high CE of 99.84% at 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> for 1 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>, suggesting that depositing Na on this matrix could achieve a stable Na metal anode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> However, in order to meet the practically useful requirement and obtain high-energy-density NMBs, anodes of high areal capacity (3 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>) would be indispensable. Considering the overall performance required for a practically useful anode, it would be valuable to take a holistic approach to solve the bottleneck problems of Na batteries.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Recently, MXene, an emerging family of 2D materials with graphene-like structure, has shown bright prospects in the field of energy storage devices, such as Li/Na-ion batteries and supercapacitors. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 36 to 38">(36−38)</a> MXene possesses a low electron/ion transport barrier owing to its open cross-linked network, thus can act as a promising substrate for alkali metal electrodeposition. Hence, its creation provides more options for the modification of Na metal anode substrates. Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>, the first one among various MXene materials, is the most common and widely used in battery research. Its advantage is the highest application potential among all MXene materials up to now and high availability on the market, which is the result of well-known and developed preparation methods. Since the wet chemical method is currently the main preparation technique of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene, the existence of a metal conductive layer and abundant terminal groups enables Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene to possess both excellent electrical conductivity (10 000 S cm<sup>–1</sup>) and hydrophilicity, which is one of the advantages over other 2D materials such as graphene. Unfortunately, the nonuniform superficial nucleation sites and inescapable self-restacking of bare MXene materials usually induce the poor reversibility of anodes during the metal deposition/dissolution process. Inspired by the synergistic superiority of a sodiophilic interphase and electrode construction, herein, a 3D Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanohybrid was developed for hosting the Na. We design Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene acting as the highly conductive substrate and g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, which is electrically less conductive, as an interfacial modulation layer to simultaneously trap Na<sup>+</sup> to electrodeposit with a controlled morphology and block the undesirable interaction between the electrolyte and anode. The 3D Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanohybrid thus made also provides a larger specific surface area, so that the local current density can be reduced to prolong the Na cyclic life. Moreover, Na ions can be confined in three-dimensional space and then electrochemically reduced to a dendrite-free metal layer, thus maintaining a little volume change during Na plating/stripping, thereby enabling the structural stability of the electrode. This work presents an integrated design of the interphase and substrate for highly stable Na metal anodes and provides a reference in the realization of advanced NMBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite was prepared through a 3D self-assembly process followed by an <i>in situ</i> calcination reaction, as schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. First, the suspension of a few-layered Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene was synthesized by the minimal intensive layer delamination method, that is, removing Al layers from a Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> precursor using the LiF/HCl etchant. The g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> precursor (urea) was then added into the MXene dispersion and mixed homogeneously after short sonication. Urea monomers will be tightly and uniformly adsorbed on the surface of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene driven by a hydrogen-bonding interaction (e.g., −NH<sub>2</sub>···O–Ti), which would be beneficial to the 3D self-assembly process of the MXene@urea nanohybrid. Lyophilization was chosen because the 3D interconnected architecture of the nanocomposite material can be well constructed by the <i>in situ</i> removal of interlayered ice with minimized structure shrinkage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> After that, urea molecules were <i>in situ</i> transformed into g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanosheets growing on the MXene surface through heat treatment, thus leading to a strong Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> heterojunction, which would be beneficial for the structural stability.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/medium/nn2c07771_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration of the fabrication process of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> in the present work.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c07771&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphology, structure, and chemical composition of the as-prepared materials were characterized using a variety of techniques. The g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> powder derived from a urea precursor at 525 °C expresses a pale yellow appearance with a low density and fluffy structure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1a</a>). As shown by the SEM image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a, the porous g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> consists of a 3D interconnected microscopic network formed by petal-like nanosheets, in which a degree of local agglomeration has occurred inevitably. The porous nanosheet structure of pure g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> was also confirmed by the TEM image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>). The Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene existed as a stable dark-green colloid solution after the etching and exfoliation processes, and its typical Tyndall scattering effect is shown in the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Pure Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene demonstrates a large-scale nanosheet morphology (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b), which would facilitate an enhancement in ionic/electronic transport. After g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> incorporation into the layer space of MXene, the nanocomposite retains a crinkled sheet-like morphology (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c) and a larger specific surface area was obtained (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). According to the SEM image and the corresponding elemental mapping images of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>), the C, Ti, N, O, and F elements evenly distribute in the sample, further indicating the MXene is uniformly coated by the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layers. The STEM image of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d,e) shows two distinct regional features (crystalline and amorphous), corresponding to the HRTEM images of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>) and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2c</a>), indicating the successful combination of the two components. As for the crystalline part, the lattice spacing of 0.26 nm was observed, and it is consistent with the (110) lattice planes of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene. Moreover, the detailed Ti atom (bright dots) distribution can be clearly seen at high magnification (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f). Furthermore, HAADF-STEM and EDS elemental maps (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>) of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite reveal that Ti, N, O, and F elements are homogeneously dispersed in the sample, further proving the successful g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> coating on the MXene surface. X-ray diffraction (XRD) characterization (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g) was used to investigate the structure and phase information on the as-prepared samples. The first point to note is that there is no corresponding peak belonging to g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> for the nanohybrid material, which proves that there is no monolithic g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> produced <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> and the layer-by-layer restacking of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> was obviously inhibited. The other point is that the intense diffraction peak of the (002) planes of the MXene at around 6.3° shifts to a lower diffraction angle, suggesting that the interlayer spacing of the MXene host is expanded, allowing for the profound penetration of the electrolyte and the effective utilization of the three-dimensional space.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/medium/nn2c07771_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Material characterizations. SEM images of (a) g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, (b) Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene, and (c) Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. (d–f) STEM images of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite. (g) XRD patterns of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene, and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. (h) High-resolution C 1s spectra of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene, and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. (j) EELS spectra of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> collected at two specific sites marked with site 1 and site 2 as indicated in the (i) STEM image.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c07771&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Then X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was employed to reveal the element composition and corresponding chemical environment in the heterostructured Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanohybrid, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h. Since the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene occupies the majority of the complex, the XPS survey spectrum of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> composite confirms the existence of Ti, C, and O, which is similar to that of bare MXene, coupled with a new peak of N 1s (6.27 at. %) originated from the introduction of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. In the fine spectra of elemental carbon (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h), the common peak at 284.8 eV is ascribed to the adventitious carbon impurities (C–C). The peak at 288.1 eV in g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> belongs to an sp<sup>2</sup>-bonded carbon from the N-containing aromatic ring (N═C–N). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The C 1s spectrum of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> displays five distinct peaks at 281.7, 284.8, 286.4, 288.3, and 289.2 eV, which can be ascribed to Ti–C, C–C, C–O, N═C–N, and O–C═O, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> It should be noted that the peak assigned to the Ti–C bonds shifts 0.2 eV to the lower binding energy region by comparison with that of MXene. In contrast, the peak belonging to N═C–N bonds in the nanohybrid displays a slight shift to higher binding energy compared to that of pure g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, indicating the formation of an interaction between MXene and the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. The intense coupling effect promotes the interfacial charge transfer between the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene and g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. The high-resolution Ti 2p and O 1s spectra of the as-prepared samples are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b,c</a>. As for the Ti 2p, the peaks located at 455.4 (460.7), 456.3 (461.7), 457.2 eV (462.7 eV), and 458.6 (464.4 eV) belong to the Ti–C, Ti(II), Ti(III), and Ti–F/TiO<sub>2–<i>x</i></sub>F<sub><i>x</i></sub> species in the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene, respectively, confirming the existence of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> in the nanocomposite. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> The high-resolution O 1s spectrum is fitted by four components, 429.9, 531.1, 532.1, and 533.3 eV, corresponding to O–Ti, C–Ti–O<sub><i>x</i></sub>, C–Ti–OH<sub><i>x</i></sub>, and adsorption water, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> As for the N 1s of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6d</a>), it could be deconvoluted into four components including the peak at 396.3 eV ascribed to Ti–N coordination on account of the strong combination between Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> and g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, while the three peaks at 398.3, 399.7, and 400.7 eV are ascribed to pyridinic N (C–N═C), graphitic N (N–(C)<sub>3</sub>), and amino groups (N–H<sub>3</sub>), respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> To further investigate the local interaction at the interphase, the spatial distribution of different species in the nanocomposite was visualized using STEM–electron energy loss spectroscopy (STEM-EELS) based on the intensity changes of the energy loss peaks. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>j, N species appear in the specific regions like site 1 rather than site 2 in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>i. Ti L energy loss edges are detected in both regions, but the intensity in site 2 is significantly lower, implying that the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene surface in this region is coated by the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer. Meanwhile, the peak of Ti L in site 1 is shifted compared with that in site 2, which may be related to the interlayer spacing and chemical environment of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> In addition, the asymmetry of the Ti L peak shape also indicates changes in chemical state and electronic structure. The above results suggest the strong coupling effect between g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene, which provides a high interconnectivity to promote the electron transfer at the heterointerface.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electronic structure and interfacial interaction of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite were analyzed by the density functional theory (DFT) calculations. Based on the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene (001) and g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (001) crystal planes, an optimized structure model of the nanohybrid with the interlayer spacing of around 3 Å is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a. Furthermore, by the charge density difference calculation, the redistribution of charges at the interphase was confirmed, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b. Specifically, the electron transfer from g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> to the highly conductive Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene makes the terminal oxygen atoms become a negatively charged center (where the yellow area represents the electron accumulation, and the cyan area represents the electron depletion on the nitrogen atoms of the tri-<i>s</i>-triazine units located in g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>), which can account for the subsequent selective Na-ion adsorption. Therefore, the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer modulates a homogeneous Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and provides a large number of Na nucleation sites at the interphase. Meanwhile, as demonstrated by the density of states (DOS) in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c, compared with the semiconducting g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite possesses higher occupied states near the Fermi level in connection with excellent electron transfer performance, which is mainly contributed by the high electrical conductivity of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene material. It can thus be concluded that the surface state of MXene has been reconstructed and the modulated interphase can not only improve the absorption of ions but also facilitates electron mobility as the electrode. The two configurations with the Na atom adsorption were constructed and optimized, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>, and the same stable adsorption structure was finally obtained, which suggests that the Na ion can easily enter the interphase of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite through the nanopores of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer due to the strong adsorption effect on N atoms in the interphase. For further clarifying the Na adsorption and deposition behaviors of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite theoretically, it is instructive to investigate the affinity between Na atoms and various substrates via adsorption energy calculations (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d). The corresponding schematic diagrams illustrating the interaction of Na atoms and Cu, bare MXene, and g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>. The MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite has the largest adsorption energy of −3.98 eV, which is significantly improved over those of the pure MXene (−0.81 eV) and g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (−3.35 eV). In addition, the adsorption energy of Cu foil for the Na atom is also relatively small (−1.15 eV). The above results show that the surface state of bare MXene has been reconstructed and the heterojunction interphase is sodiophilic and conducive to the capture and nucleation of Na atoms, contributing to the morphological stability of Na deposition. Metal electrodeposition is significantly influenced by the electric field distribution at/near the electrode/electrolyte interphase. COMSOL Multiphysics was conducted to perform the current density simulation to elucidate the potential Na deposition behavior. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e,f, the cross-sectional structure of the working electrode with protrusions on the surface was used to mimic the bare Cu substrate. Likewise, a rational simplification of the 3D Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> model was put into effect to cut down on the numerous calculations during simulation. In the chart of the electric field distribution, the color transition from blue to red represents the relative current density changes from low to high. The results in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e show that the electric field strength is the highest at the protrusion center (red region), displaying the so-called “tip effect”. As a consequence, the trajectory of Na ions in the electrolyte is forced to concentrate at the tip, leading to an uneven nucleation and deposition on the Cu substrate. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> In other words, Na metal tends to plate/strip on/from these regions, leading to a longitudinal Na growth pathway and inevitable dendrite issues. Conversely, a layer of the nanoporous ion channels mimicking Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> was introduced to the protrusion-bearing electrode surface, and as such, the electric field was readjusted to reduce the likelihood of hot spots. Hence, the Na-ion distribution within the electrolyte exhibits such a homogeneous concentration region that ions can be evenly fluxed through the interphase, enabling a dendrite-free electrodeposition.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/medium/nn2c07771_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Optimized structure of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. The brown, gray, red, and blue balls denote C, N, O, and Ti atoms, respectively. (b) Simulation of the charge density differences of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> interphase. (c) Density of states of bare g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (blue), bare MXene (red), and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (gray). (d) DFT calculations on binding energy of a Na atom with different substrates. COMSOL Multiphysics simulations at the electrolyte/electrode interphase of (e) Cu foil and (f) the MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> hybrid.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c07771&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the important role of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> heterojunction interphase in inhibiting Na dendrites, a SEM study was carried out to visually monitor the plating/stripping morphologies for the different amounts of Na on the substrates. The initial surface statuses of the contrast electrode (bare Cu foil) and the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a and e. The former has a mass of protrusions on the 2D surface, while the latter exhibits a microscopic 3D porous structure. SEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f,g and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9c,d</a>) show that the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> substrate exhibits a progressively compact surface with increasing Na deposition time (ranging from 1 to 6 h at 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>), and no large Na agglomeration can be observed. The surface of the 3D Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode remains smooth without Na dendrites or bulk Na agglomerates even at the high capacity of 3 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>, well due to the homogenization in nucleation sites by the presence of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer. Although there are many sodiophilic sites in the structure of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, which help to alleviate the uneven deposition of Na, aggregation and nonuniform distribution of Na metal are still observed (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a,b</a>). The possible reason for local highlighting is the poor conductivity of the material or the charging caused by the coverage of “dead Na”, which leads to local charge accumulation. The SEM images of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> electrode after plating Na of 3 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10c,d</a>) display an irregular deposition morphology.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/medium/nn2c07771_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. SEM images of Na metal deposited on a Cu electrode after plating (a) 0 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>, (b) 0.5 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>, and (c) 1 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>. SEM images of Na metal deposited on (d) a Cu electrode after 50 cycles. SEM images of Na metal deposited on an MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode after plating (e) 0 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>, (f) 0.5 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>, and (g) 1 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>. SEM images of Na metal deposited on (h) an MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode after 50 cycles. (i) AIMD simulation results of Na-ion diffusion kinetics in the MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> heterointerface. Schematic illustration of the Na plating behaviors on (j) bare Cu foil and (k) an MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c07771&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In addition, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode surface does not show any obvious changes after repeated plating/stripping processes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>h), implying a good structural stability and uniform Na ion flux, which can ensure the desired stable Na plating/stripping cycling. On further stripping the Na metal from the plated electrode, a morphology similar to that of the original electrode emerges, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11j,k</a>, accompanied by extremely less residual Na particles, implying that the electrode processes excellent cycling efficiency. In contrast, when the Na metal was stripped from g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene, the surfaces of both substrates were covered with a large amount of residual Na (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11d,e,g,h</a>), resulting in irreversible Na loss, which will lead to a rapid decrease in capacity. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>, as the current density and areal capacity increased up to 5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> and 10 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>, lots of Na dendrites can be observed on the Cu foil substrate (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12a,b</a>). In contrast, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> substrate displayed a uniform surface without any Na dendrites (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12c,d</a>). This can be ascribed to the homogeneous Na<sup>+</sup> flux and excellent sodiophilicity of the 3D hetero-interphase, which guarantees a long cycling life and high reversibility of Na metal anodes.</div><div class="NLM_p">In parallel, an <i>ab initio</i> molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulation was performed to demonstrate the diffusion dynamics of a Na ion crossing the heterointerface. Four snapshots (0, 60, 120, and 180 fs) of this motion process are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>i. It is evident that the Na atom progressively diffuses into the heterostructured Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> interphase and the entire Na atom has passed through the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer and was then adsorbed upon the surface of MXene after an AIMD simulation of 180 fs. Cross sections of Na metal (10 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>) deposited Cu, MXene, g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrodes were characterized to reveal the change of electrode thickness (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13–S15</a>). As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>, the thickness of the electrode after Na deposition is about 150 μm due to the absence of 3D pore space of planar Cu foil for Na storage. Similarly, the thickness of the Na deposition layer on the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene substrate reaches 159 μm. It can be seen that although the growth of Na dendrites was relieved compared with that of the Cu foil surface, the inhibition effect of the volume change was not significant because of the small specific surface area of the bare Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene. The huge volume change would destroy the SEI film on the Na metal surface, further leading to severe side reactions and an uneven Na deposition process. For the bare g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> substrate (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>), the Na deposition will penetrate deeply into its three-dimensional space, so a smaller thickness (108 μm) is obtained with the same amount of Na deposition. The disadvantage of this electrode is the poor conductivity, so it is easy to cause the accumulation of “dead Na” in the long-term cycling, which increases the internal polarization markedly and eventually leads the battery to fail. Interestingly, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> substrate achieves a thickness of 99 μm after Na plating (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>), which is the smallest value among all substrates. The overall morphology of the electrode remained intact, and no Na dendrites can be found, indicating the uniform and conformal Na deposition on the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> substrate. The above results indicate that the three-dimensional sodiophilic structure is beneficial for the storage and subsequent long-term cycling of Na metal. Cross-sectional SEM images of Na metal deposited Cu foil and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> substrates after 50 cycles further reveal the accumulation of “dead Na”. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16a</a>, the deposited Na expresses a rugged microstructure with a thick “dead Na” layer, and Na dendrites grew on the electrochemically inactive surface. In contrast, there seems to be no obvious “dead Na” and dendrites on the surface of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> substrate in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16b</a>, indicating that the formation of “dead Na” was inhibited.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>k schematically illustrates the deposition behavior of Na on the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode. Specifically, the 3D network structure can reduce the local current density, alleviate the volume change, and maintain the structural stability of the electrode. More importantly, the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer with abundant nanopores can provide uniform Na-ion diffusion channels and induce uniform Na nucleation and deposition. Furthermore, the intrinsically disordered g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer avoids the effect of grain boundaries on dendrite growth. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> In contrast, the Na deposition behavior shows a huge difference on the Cu electrode, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b,c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a,b</a>. After plating a small areal capacity of 0.5 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>, Na deposition would aggregate severely in local regions, resulting in the formation of a rough surface. When the deposition capacity reached 3 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>, obvious pores appear on the surface of the plated Na, and the electrode volume expansion is serious. In addition, when the half cell was recharged, a large amount of Na metal still covered the Cu surface and could not reproduce the original morphology (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11a,b</a>). Furthermore, after 50 cycles of Na deposition/dissolution (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d), the local longitudinal growth of Na metal was aggravated, which could pierce the separator and cause an internal short circuit, resulting in the failure of the battery. Likewise, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>j visualizes the deposition behavior of Na metal on the 2D Cu foil. For a bare Cu substrate, the inhomogeneity in the surface causes the anisotropy of the electric field and ion distribution, which in turn affects the deposition behavior of Na metal. Local excessive deposition leads to the rupture of the SEI, induces uncontrolled dendrite growth, and destroys the electrochemical performance of the battery.</div><div class="NLM_p">To verify the DFT calculation studies, the nucleation overpotentials of Na metal on different substrates were evaluated with 1 M sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) in diglyme as the electrolyte, where the nucleation overpotential has been defined in previous investigations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, at the current density of 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode exhibits a strong sodiophilicity with the smallest Na nucleation overpotential (9 mV), followed by MXene (14 mV) and g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> (20 mV), while Na possesses the largest overpotential value (34 mV) on the Cu foil surface, signifying the smallest nucleation energy barrier of Na metal deposition on the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> substrate, which is responsible for a uniform deposition morphology and is in line with the DFT calculation results in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d. The advantages of the heterojunction interphase offer an opportunity to improve the Coulombic efficiency and cycling stability of Na metal anodes. Asymmetric cell tests were conducted using various substrates as the working electrodes and Na foil as the counter electrode. The measured results at different current densities and areal capacities are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b,c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S17 and S18</a>.
