At the current density of 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> with a capacity limitation of 1 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b), the MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode can be cycled stably for 400 cycles and displays a high average CE of 99.9%, which is markedly superior when compared with the reference electrodes (i.e., MXene, g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, and Cu foil electrodes in the present work). The relatively low CE in the initial few cycles can well be caused by the activation of the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer and the reduction of residual oxygen-containing species on the electrode surface. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> When the current density was raised to 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, the CEs of the Cu half cell fluctuated severely in the whole process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c). Under the same condition, the MXene electrode also displayed rather unstable CEs and could not maintain a regular working status after 100 cycles. Likewise, the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> half cell expressed a deteriorative CE for less than 80 cycles due to its high energy barrier for the initial Na nucleation and poor electronic conductivity. The poor cycling stability could well be linked to the uneven plating/stripping of Na on the substrate, which deteriorated the electrode/electrolyte interphase to induce side reactions between Na and the electrolyte. The electrically disconnected Na was accumulated in abundance, resulting in an increase in the irreversible capacity. In sharp contrast, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode exhibited a considerably stable cycling performance with a high CE at ∼99.7% for 300 cycles. Furthermore, when tested at a higher current density of 3 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, an excellent CE of as high as 99.7% was still achieved for the MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode for 200 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>). In particular, even when cycled with a high capacity of 3 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup> at the current density of 3 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>, the MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode still maintained good cycling stability for 100 cycles, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>. The above results confirm that the heterojunction interphase with outstanding sodiophilicity provides abundant Na nucleation sites and deposition space. Hence, side reactions between Na and the electrolyte, dendrite growth, and volume changes were significantly suppressed, leading to the much improved reversibility of Na metal anodes, which is vitally important to high capacity retention of full cells. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> In addition, the voltage profiles obtained in the galvanostatic charge–discharge cycling test for the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d. From an enlarged view of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e, it can be clearly observed that during cycling the voltage hysteresis increases slowly, and the voltage difference is located in a small range of 7–23 mV, indicating the excellent reaction kinetics of the cells.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/medium/nn2c07771_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Nucleation overpotentials of Na on Cu, g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>, MXene, and MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrodes at 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>. Cycling performance comparison of different electrodes with current densities of (b) 0.5 and (c) 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> for a total 1 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup> of Na. (d) Voltage profiles of the MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode at different cycles at 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> for 1 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>. (e) Partially enlarged view for the voltage profiles at 0.5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> for 1 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup> on the MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c07771&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Likewise, symmetric cells were fabricated using Na, Cu@Na, g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@Na, and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@Na as both the working and counter electrodes, to assess the application potential of Na composite anodes. At the current density of 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> and areal capacity of 1 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>, for the pure Na anode, the voltage polarization is small in the initial few cycles, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a. However, due to the serious side reactions on the surface of Na metal, the voltage polarization increases rapidly over 50 cycles (100 h), and there is a sudden drop after 160 h, which means that a short circuit may well have occurred on account of the dendrite growth inside the battery. The cell based on bare Cu suffers from large voltage polarization due to the poor electrode/electrolyte interphase and displays an abnormal voltage profile after 370 h because of the spatial variation of Na plating/stripping. In addition, the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@Na symmetric cell exhibited a large and erratic voltage hysteresis during cycling, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a–c. This phenomenon indicated that the electrode/electrolyte interphase was unstable and side reactions were serious. Among the four cells, the cell using the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode exhibits a much lower overpotential (11 mV) and realizes highly stable operation throughout the whole cycling process (700 h). The corresponding details of voltage profiles for different cells are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b–d, in which flat voltage plateaus for the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode indicates a more stable electrode/electrolyte interphase. A similar judgment can also be drawn from the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements after various cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>). Again, the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> electrode exhibits a small interfacial resistance with negligible change after cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19a</a>), demonstrating the improved interfacial stability. Compared with the composite electrode, the interfacial impedance of pure Na symmetric cells increases significantly during cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19b</a>), which is closely related to the aggregation of the “dead Na” and dendrite growth caused by inhomogeneous Na deposition. Furthermore, the rate performances of symmetrical bare Na, Cu@Na, and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@Na cells were compared at the current density ranging from 0.5 to 5 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> at a fixed capacity of 1 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup> in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a>. Compared with Cu@Na, the lower conductivity and larger interfacial impedance of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> result in a bit higher voltage hysteresis during the initial cycling process under a small current density. 3D Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> provides a higher ionic conductivity due to its high electrolyte uptake. Meanwhile, the lower interfacial impedance stemming from fewer side reactions and large specific surface area play a decisive role in stabilizing the anode/electrolyte interphase especially under high current densities, so the polarization is smaller than those of the contrast electrodes in the prolonged cycling process. Although the voltage polarization inevitably increases gradually as the current density increases, it can be well maintained at small values (&lt;34 mV). However, the voltage polarizations of the cells with the control electrodes enlarge greatly with the step increase in current density, which could well be attributed to the enhanced increasing rate of inert species and dendrites on the Na metal surface. It is noteworthy that the Coulombic efficiency and cycle life of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> are superior to other hosts reported for Na metal (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>).</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/medium/nn2c07771_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Voltage profiles of Na plating/stripping in three styles of symmetric cells, that is, Na foil, Cu@Na, g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@Na, and MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@Na cells at 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> for 1 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>. (b–d) Detailed voltage profiles with time indicated in (a). (e) Cycling performance of full cells at 2 C. (f) Rate performance of the full cells.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/asset/images/large/nn2c07771_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c07771&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The composition of the SEI was disclosed using XPS depth analysis to investigate the influence of the SEI for Na plating/stripping behaviors. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21a,b</a> show the variation trends of carbon and fluorine elements from the surface to the interior of the SEI film. With the increase of Ar<sup>+</sup> sputtering time, the content of carbon decreased rapidly, while the content of fluorine first increased to a higher level and then decreased gradually. The carbon is mainly related to the organic component formed by the reduction of the electrolyte solvent. Thus, the decrease of the carbon component means that the reaction of Na metal with the electrolyte is inhibited. The fluorine element on the initial surface mainly comes from the P–F bond (686.0 eV), which is related to the residues of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PF<sub><i>y</i></sub> and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> After 60 s of sputtering, the fluorine content increased significantly to 15.58 at. %, which was mainly contributed by the reduction product (Na–F, 684.4 eV) of NaPF<sub>6</sub>. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21c</a> shows the change of the main element content of the SEI film during the etching process. The depth of the 60 s etching is about 10 nm in this work, so the thickness of the outer layer of the SEI film is about 10 nm, which consists of massive organic components. Inorganic species such as NaF and Na–O account for a relatively higher proportion in the interior (below 10 nm) of the SEI. The effect of the SEI film on the performance of Na metal anodes is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21d</a>. The inorganic components in the SEI film help to prevent the penetration of the electrolyte solvent, thereby avoiding side reactions and effectively reducing the excessive consumption of Na metal and electrolyte, which is beneficial to the subsequent uniform Na plating/stripping.</div><div class="NLM_p last">To further demonstrate the application potential of the sodiophilic heterostructured interphase in Na metal batteries, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>-Na batteries were assembled using the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> with Na deposition (3 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>) as the anode. Since the load of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> is about 0.8 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>, the theoretical capacity of the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> can be calculated as 15.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, and this composite anode with 3 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup> of Na could deliver a high theoretical gravimetric capacity of 892 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 76.5% of the theoretical capacity (1166 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) of the Na metal anode. The homemade NVP cathode was fabricated by a convenient solution evaporation method, followed by thermal treatments, and the morphology and phase structure of the as-prepared samples were characterized using scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and XRD (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S22</a>). The charge/discharge profiles of NVP||Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@Na full cells at various cycles and rates further verify the excellent reversibility and cycling stability of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@Na, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771/suppl_file/nn2c07771_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S23</a>. In addition, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e displays the long-term cycling performances of the full cells assembled with the different Na metal anodes. Among the anodes studied in the present work, the NVP||Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@Na full cell exhibited an outstanding cycling capability, which delivered a discharge capacity of 88 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 800 cycles at 2 C. The capacity retention at a rate of 2 C decreased from 92.4% for Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@Na (800 cycles), to 91.4% for Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@Na (250 cycles), to 88.7% for Cu@Na (156 cycles), and to 81.8% for bare Na (83 cycles). Moreover, the NVP||Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@Na full cell also demonstrated a promising rate performance of up to 95, 92, 86, and 78 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2, 5, 10, and 20 C, respectively, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>f. In contrast, although the NVP||Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@Na full cell tested in the same conditions exhibited comparable capacity at low rate, the capacity faded quickly with the rate. Likewise, the absence of the sodiophilic interphase of Cu@Na and Na makes the full cells poorer in rate capability. These results further confirm that the excellent heterostructured interfacial stability renders the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> a competitive anode host for full cells.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have successfully constructed a multifunctional Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> heterointerface that possesses tuned electronic properties, strong sodiophilicity, and structural robustness, made by a 3D self-assembly process followed by an <i>in situ</i> calcination reaction for Na metal anodes. A high Coulombic efficiency and dendrite-free Na plating/stripping without a parasitic reaction were achieved, where the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>@Na electrode not only demonstrated excellent long cycling stability and low voltage hysteresis for symmetric cells but also delivered an excellent cycle stability and outstanding rate capability of the full cells. These results indicate an enormous potential of the sodiophilic nanocomposite acting as the host for Na metal by the feat of insulating/conducting heterostructures. The advantages of this design can be summarized as follows: (i) the amorphous g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer modulates the surface state of MXene and enables a high homogeneity of the interphase, (ii) the sodiophilic interphase induces uniform Na-ion transport and nucleation, and (iii) the 3D interconnected construction attenuates the volume change and lowers the local current density for a much raised rate capability. This work provides important insights for the practical application of Na metal anodes.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Methods</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Preparation of Delaminated Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> Nanosheets</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene nanosheets were prepared by selectively removing Al layers from Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> MAX phase powders according to what has been reported in the literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> First, 1.6 g of lithium fluoride (LiF, 99.98%, Sigma-Aldrich) was added to 20 mL of 9.0 M hydrochloric acid (HCl) solution under vigorous stirring until it was completely dissolved. Then 1 g of the MAX phase material (purchased from Beijing Forsman Scientific Co. Ltd.) was slowly added into the solution under stirring. Subsequently, the mixture was kept at 35 °C for 24 h at a stirring rate of 450 rpm. Afterward, the acidic mixture was washed repeatedly using deionized water by centrifugation until the pH reached ∼6. To obtain a single-layer or few-layer MXene solution, the multilayered suspension was ultrasonicated for 30 min under a N<sub>2</sub> environment in the ice bath. The supernatant was collected as the final delaminated MXene product after centrifugation at 3500 rpm for 30 min.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Preparation of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> Composites</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite was synthesized by processes involving three-dimensional self-assembly and an <i>in situ</i> calcination reaction. Typically, 0.2 g of urea (ACS reagent, Sigma-Aldrich) was added into 50 mL of a Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> aqueous suspension (∼2 mg mL<sup>–1</sup>) under vigorous stirring, followed by sonication for 30 min to obtain a uniform solution. After that, the collected solution was frozen using liquid nitrogen and freeze-dried for 2 days to obtain the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@urea nanocomposite. The 3D self-assembly process enables a uniform distribution of urea on the surface of MXene due to the hydrogen-bonding interaction between the amine groups in the urea and the O/F-containing functional groups of MXene. The Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@urea nanocomposite was then annealed at 525 °C (ramping rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>) for 2 h under an Ar environment in a tube furnace (Thermo Scientific). During this process, urea was <i>in situ</i> condensed into g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> on the surface of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene, obtaining the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> nanocomposite. In addition, bare g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> was also obtained without MXene incorporation through the same procedure.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Preparation of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> Cathodes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All of the chemicals with analytical grade were purchased from Sigma-Aldrich. Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) was synthesized by a facile solution evaporation method, followed by calcination. Typically, 10 mmol of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (anhydrous, ACS reagent), 6.7 mmol of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (99.95%, trace metals basis), 20 mmol of NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, and 10 mmol of citric acid were successively added into 50 mL of deionized water and kept stirring for 2 h. The molar ratios of V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, and citric acid were controlled at 1:1.5:3:1.5. Subsequently, the above solution was heated at 80 °C to evaporate the water under constant string. The lime-green residues thus obtained were ground in a mortar and then transferred to a tube furnace. The powders were preheated at 350 °C for 4 h and then calcined at 800 °C for 12 h in an Ar atmosphere to produce the final Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> phase.</div></div><div id="sec4_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Na plating/stripping and CE characterization were conducted by the galvanostatic charge–discharge experiments with half cells. CR2025-type coin cells were assembled in an Ar atmosphere glovebox using various hosts as the working electrode, Na foil as the counter and the reference electrode, and a piece of Celgard 2400 polypropylene film (25 μm thickness) as the separator. The adopted electrolyte was 1 M sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) in 2-methoxyethyl ether (diglyme) without any additives, and the usage amount was strictly controlled at 50 μL for each cell to standardize the testing. Working electrodes were then fabricated by the typical slurry-making process. The mass ratios of active materials (Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>), PVDF, and carbon black were controlled at 5:1:0.5. They were mixed in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) solvent under constant stirring to form a homogeneous slurry, and then the slurry was coated onto Cu foil by doctor blading, followed by vacuum drying at 80 °C for 12 h. Current collectors were cut into round pieces (diameter of 12 mm), and the Na foil diameter was controlled at 14 mm. For symmetrical batteries, a pair of identical electrodes with predeposited Na (3 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>) were matched to evaluate the cycling stability and voltage fluctuation of the electrodes at 1 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup> at 1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup> using a Neware battery testing system. The EIS was performed using a Solartron 1470E electrochemical workstation with a frequency range from 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz. NVP cathodes were prepared by uniformly mixing the active materials, PVDF, and carbon black with a weight ratio of 8:1:1 in NMP solvent and coating the slurry onto Al foil, followed by vacuum drying at 120 °C overnight. Afterward, the full cells were assembled with homemade NVP electrodes (loaded ∼2.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>) as the cathode, and various substrates with predeposited Na (3 mA h cm<sup>–2</sup>) as the anodes, and 50 μL of electrolyte. Similar to the case of the symmetrical cell assembly, the electrode with predeposited Na was obtained by disassembling from the half cells. The full cells were cycled within the voltage range of 2.5 to 3.8 V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). All the electrochemical measurements were carried out at room temperature.</div></div><div id="sec4_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Structure Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The morphologies and structures of the as-prepared samples were studied using a scanning electron microscope (Zeiss Supra 40) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM, JEOL-2100F). Elemental composition and distribution of the samples were detected by energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS). STEM and EELS measurements were conducted using a JEOL ARM200F at 80 kV equipped with an ASCOR aberration corrector, cold field emission gun, and Gatan Quantum ER spectrometer. XRD patterns were recorded using a Bruker D8 diffractometer at 40 kV and 40 mA (Cu K radiation of 0.154 06 nm) to probe the crystal structures of the samples. XPS spectra were collected on a Kratos Analytical Axis Ultra DLD UHV spectrometer equipped with an Al Kα X-ray source (1486.6 eV). N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms were measured using a Micromeritics 3FLEX at 77 K, where the specific surface area was calculated using the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method. Cycled substrates depositing different amounts of Na metal were also studied using SEM to monitor the morphological evolution of Na plating/stripping on the substrates.</div></div><div id="sec4_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Computational Details</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The DFT calculations were conducted under the Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP) code. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> The interaction between ion and electron was performed by the projector-augmented wave (PAW) method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> The electronic exchange and correlation were calculated by the Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) method, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> and the cutoff energy was set to 500 eV. In addition, considering that the weak interactions cannot be described accurately using the standard PBE functional, empirical atom-pairwise corrections proposed by the DFT-D3 scheme with Becke–Jonson damping were applied to describe the long-range van der Waals interactions. For the geometry optimization and the calculation of adsorption energy, all atoms were fully relaxed until the energies and residual forces on each atom converged to 10<sup>–5</sup> eV and 0.01 eV Å<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. One whole layer of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (001) slab was selected as the clean surface of MXene, and the structure model of g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> was built in a monolayer system. The Brillouin zone was sampled using the Monkhorst–Pack scheme with the sizes of 4 × 4 × 1 <i>k</i>-meshes for all calculations in the MXene structure and the sizes of one Γ <i>k</i>-point for all systems containing g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>. A 20 Å vacuum along the <i>z</i>-direction was utilized to prevent the possible interaction between periodically repeated structural boxes. Spin polarizations were also taken into consideration in the computations. The Nose-Hoover thermostat method was used to perform the AIMD simulations to analyze the possible pathway for sodium-ion crossing through the g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> layer. The adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>b</sub>) of the Na ion on the MXene clean surface was calculated according to<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">b</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>total</mi></mrow></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>sub</mi></mrow></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>Na</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"></div></div>where <i>E</i><sub>total</sub>, <i>E</i><sub>sub</sub>, and <i>E</i><sub>Na</sub> are the respective energies of the complex model bound with Na<sup>+</sup>, the complex surface, and a Na atom in a sodium crystal.</div></div><div id="sec4_7" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Numerical Simulation</h3><div class="NLM_p">Simulations of the Na-ion concentration and current density distributions in this work were carried out using COMSOL Multiphysics 5.4 software. The physical modulus adopted was “tertiary current distribution”. The Na<sup>+</sup> deposition process was simulated by coupling the Nernst–Planck equation with the Butler–Volmer equation. The Nernst–Planck equations are used in these simulations:<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq2"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>N</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>D</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">∇</mi><msub><mrow><mi>c</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>z</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mi>u</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></msub><mi mathvariant="italic">F</mi><msub><mrow><mi>c</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">∇</mi><msub><mrow><mi>φ</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"></div></div><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq3"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mi>∂</mi><msub><mrow><mi>c</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><mi>∂</mi><mi>t</mi></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mo>−</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">∇</mi><msub><mrow><mi>N</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"></div></div><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq4"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><munder><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">∑</mo></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></munder><msub><mrow><mi>z</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mi>c</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mn>0</mn></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"></div></div>where <i>N</i><sub>i</sub> is the flux vector of the species, <i>D</i><sub>i</sub> is the diffusion coefficient, <i>c</i><sub>i</sub> is ion concentration, <i>z</i><sub>i</sub> is the ion charge number, <i>u</i><sub>i</sub> is the ion mobility, and φ<sub>i</sub> is the electric field.</div><div class="NLM_p">The reaction kinetics at the electrode surface was described using the Butler–Volmer equation:<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq5"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>i</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>0</mn></mrow></msub><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><msub><mrow><mi>C</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">R</mi></mrow></msub><mspace width=".25em"></mspace><mi>exp</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow></msub><mi mathvariant="italic">F</mi><mi>η</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>R</mi><mi>T</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>C</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi></mrow></msub><mspace width=".25em"></mspace><mi>exp</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi></mrow></msub><mi mathvariant="italic">F</mi><mi>η</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>R</mi><mi>T</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"></div></div>where <i>i</i><sub>0</sub> is the exchange current density, <i>C</i><sub>R</sub> and <i>C</i><sub>O</sub> are the dimensionless concentrations of the reduced and oxidized species, α<sub>a</sub> and α<sub>c</sub> are the anodic and cathodic charge transfer coefficients, η is the activation overpotential, <i>R</i> is the gas constant, and <i>T</i> is the temperature.</div><div class="NLM_p last">A two-dimensional model (20 × 15 μm<sup>2</sup>) was first established to display the entire geometry construction consisting of two electrodes (20 μm in length), and the distance between them was 15 μm (this part was occupied by the bulk electrolyte). The humps on the Cu electrode surface were mimicked by three semicircles with a diameter of 2 μm and a height of 3 μm, and the gap between two humps was set as 3 μm. The MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>/electrolyte interphase is composed of ion channels (0.1 μm in diameter) attached on MXene nanosheets. It should be noted that this module can only establish an ideal system due to the model simplification for the calculation. The Na ion conductivity of the electrolyte was set as 0.5 S m<sup>–1</sup>, the initial concentration was 1 M, the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> was 1.59 × 10<sup>–10</sup>, and the applied voltage of deposition was 0.1 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. To acquire high-quality calculation results, the simulation model is built by using ultrafine grid division.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i17">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsnano.2c07771</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Digital images of the samples; TEM images of bare g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> and MXene; N<sub>2</sub> adsorption and desorption isotherms of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub> composite; SEM image and the corresponding elemental mapping of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>; HAADF-STEM image and the corresponding elemental mapping of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub> MXene@g-C<sub>3</sub>N<sub>4</sub>;         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With the urgent market demand for high-energy-d. batteries, the alloy-type or conversion-type anodes with high specific capacity have gained increasing attention to replace current low-specific-capacity graphite-based anodes.  However, alloy-type and conversion-type anodes have large initial irreversible capacity compared with graphite-based anodes, which consume most of the Li+ in the corresponding cathode and severely reduces the energy d. of full cells.  Therefore, for the practical application of these high-capacity anodes, it is urgent to develop a com. available prelithiation technique to compensate for their large initial irreversible capacity.  At present, various prelithiation methods for compensating the initial irreversible capacity of the anode have been reported, but due to their resp. shortcomings, large-scale com. applications have not yet been achieved.  In this review, we have systematically summarized and analyzed the advantages and challenges of various prelithiation methods, providing enlightenment for the further development of each prelithiation strategy toward commercialization and thus facilitating the practical application of high-specific-capacity anodes in the next-generation high-energy-d. lithium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGplmLxc_cxFibVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljSa0rk2RPmUQ"> &gt;&gt;         </div><div class="casAbstract">An efficient integration of electrochromic and electrochem. devices into one flexible entity enables both energy storage and energy-saving dual-functionalities. The smart window made of the FDED enables a large temp. difference of 27.6°C confirm-tested in model houses, where the energy source also powers three light-emitting diodes (LEDs).                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Seamless alloying stabilizes solid-electrolyte interphase for highly reversible lithium metal anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jiang, Yunpeng; Lv, Qiang; Bao, Changyuan; Wang, Bo; Ren, Penghui; Zhong, Haoyin; Yang, Yi; Liu, Ximeng; Dong, Yichao; Jin, Fan; Wang, Dianlong; Xiong, Ting; Liu, Huakun; Dou, Shixue; Wang, John; Xue, Junmin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Cell Reports Physical Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Despite their widespread study, lithium metal anodes still face the bottleneck problem of low av. Coulombic efficiency. The uniform nanoscale silver layer is conducive to seamless Li/Ag alloying process, thereby enabling flat lithium deposition.  Since the Li/Ag alloying process occurs after the formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), the seamless alloying process stabilizes the SEI, which improves the reversibility of the lithium metal anode.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium metal anodes: Emerging solutions to dendrite growth</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Byeongyong; Paek, Eunsu; Mitlin, David; Lee, Seung Woo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Reviews (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">119</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5416-5460</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CHREAY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0009-2665</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">This comprehensive Review focuses on the key challenges and recent progress regarding sodium-metal anodes employed in sodium-metal batteries (SMBs).  The metal anode is the essential component of emerging energy storage systems such as sodium sulfur and sodium selenium, which are discussed as example full-cell applications.  We begin with a description of the differences in the chem. and phys. properties of Na metal vs. the oft-studied Li metal, and a corresponding discussion regarding the no. of ways in which Na does not follow Li-inherited paradigms in its electrochem. behavior.  We detail the major challenges for Na-metal systems that at this time limit the feasibility of SMBs.  The core Na anode problems are the following interrelated degrdn. mechanisms: An unstable solid electrolyte interphase with most org. electrolytes, "mossy" and "lath-like" metal dendrite growth for liq. systems, poor Coulombic efficiency, and gas evolution.  Even solid-state Na batteries are not immune, with metal dendrites being reported.  The solns. may be subdivided into the following interrelated taxonomy: Improved electrolytes and electrolyte additives tailored for Na-metal anodes, interfacial engineering between the metal and the liq. or solid electrolyte, electrode architectures that both reduce the c.d. during plating-stripping and serve as effective hosts that shield the Na metal from excessive reactions, and alloy design to tune the bulk properties of the metal per se.  For instance, stable plating-stripping of Na is extremely difficult with conventional carbonate solvents but has been reported with ethers and glymes.  Solid-state electrolytes (SSEs) such as beta-alumina solid electrolyte (BASE), sodium superionic conductor (NASICON), and sodium thiophosphate (75Na2S·25P2S5) present highly exciting opportunities for SMBs that avoid the dangers of flammable liqs.  Even SSEs are not immune to dendrites, however, which grow through the defects in the bulk pellet, but may be controlled through interfacial energy modification.  We conclude with a discussion of the key research areas that we feel are the most fruitful for further pursuit.  In our opinion, greatly improved understanding and control of the SEI structure is the key to cycling stability.  A holistic approach involving complementary post-mortem, in situ, and operando analyses to elucidate full battery cell level structure-performance relations is advocated.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqQ1buYt3eMx7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhf9FeTXOf9tA"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Boosting electrochemical kinetics of S cathodes for room temperature Na/S batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Fan; Wang, Bo; Wang, Jiulin; Wang, Yunxiao; Ning, Yu; Yang, Jing; Zhang, Zekun; Liu, Peng; Zhou, Yu; Wang, Dianlong; Liu, Huakun; Dou, Shixue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Matter</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Inc.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Due to the low cost and high energy d., the room temp. (RT) Na/S battery system is deemed as one of the best potential energy-storage systems.  However, various issues still severely hinder its practical applications, esp. the shuttle effect caused by sol. sodium polysulfides (NaPSs) and the low conversion efficiency of S8 and Na2S2/Na2S in S cathodes.  Recently, various strategies were adopted into RT-Na/S batteries to boost the electrochem. kinetics of S cathodes, which are capable of significantly accelerating (or altering) the redox process and mitigating the shuttle effect of S cathodes.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent advanced skeletons in sodium metal anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chu, Chenxiao; Li, Rui; Cai, Feipeng; Bai, Zhongchao; Wang, Yunxiao; Xu, Xun; Wang, Nana; Yang, Jian; Dou, ShiXue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The sodium metal anode exhibits great potential in next-generation high-energy-d. batteries due to its high theor. capacity (1165 mA h g-1) at low redox potential (-2.71 V vs. std. hydrogen electrode) as well as the high natural abundance and low cost of Na resources.  However, its practical application in rechargeable batteries is hindered by uncontrollable dendrite growth that leads to poor coulombic efficiency, short lifespan, infinite vol. change and even safety issues during plating/stripping processes.  Among various strategies, the application of skeletons for Na metal anodes demonstrates a pos. influence on reducing local c.d., inhibiting dendrite growth, and alleviating vol. expansion.  This work reviews the research progress of various skeleton materials for sodium metal anodes in recent years, including carbon-based skeletons, alloy-based skeletons, metallic skeletons and MXene-based skeletons.  Simultaneously, the recent technol. advances and strategies are summarized and categorized.  Finally, we discuss the development prospects and research strategies of skeleton materials in sodium metal anodes from the perspective of basic research and practical applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrbE0USdpbtlrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhf9FeTXOf9tA"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Design Strategies to Enable the Efficient Use of Sodium Metal Anodes in High-Energy Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Bing; Xiong, Pan; Maitra, Urmimala; Langsdorf, Daniel; Yan, Kang; Wang, Chengyin; Janek, Juergen; Schroeder, Daniel; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">32</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-based batteries have attracted considerable attention and are recognized as ideal candidates for large-scale and low-cost energy storage.  Sodium (Na) metal anodes are considered as one of the most promising anodes for next-generation, high-energy, Na-based batteries owing to their high theor. specific capacity (1166 mA h g-1) and low std. electrode potential.  Herein, an overview of the recent developments in Na metal anodes for high-energy batteries is provided.  The high reactivity and large vol. expansion of Na metal anodes during charge and discharge make the electrode/electrolyte interphase unstable, leading to the formation of Na dendrites, short cycle life, and safety issues.  Design strategies to enable the efficient use of Na metal anodes are elucidated, including liq. electrolyte engineering, electrode/electrolyte interface optimization, sophisticated electrode construction, and solid electrolyte engineering.  Finally, the remaining challenges and future research directions are identified.  It is hoped that this progress report will shape a consistent view of this field and provide inspiration for future research to improve Na metal anodes and enable the development of high-energy sodium batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqzjTrnnlAmYrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtvgTIuO_vIA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXhvFGhurfI&amp;md5=29c0356a4c9e4fb466d90b6c3c8c528b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202004891"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Solid Electrolyte Interphases on Sodium Metal Anodes</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Solid Electrolyte Interphases on Sodium Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bao, Changyuan; Wang, Bo; Liu, Peng; Wu, Hao; Zhou, Yu; Wang, Dianlong; Liu, Huakun; Dou, Shixue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">30</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">52</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2004891</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium metal anodes have attracted significant attention due to their high specific capacity (1166 mA h g-1), low redox potential (-2.71 V vs the std. hydrogen electrode), and abundant natural resources.  Nevertheless, unstable solid electrolyte interphases (SEI) and uncontrolled dendrite growth critically hinder their commercialization.  Notably, SEIs play a crit. role in regulating Na deposition and improving the cycling stability of rechargeable Na metal batteries.  Recently, SEI research on Na metal anodes has been intensively conducted worldwide; thus, a comprehensive review is necessary.  Herein, initially, the fundamentals of SEI and the related issues induced by its intrinsic instability are discussed.  Thereafter, advanced characterization techniques that unveil the morphol. evolution and interfacial chem. of Na metal anodes are presented.  Subsequently, efficient strategies, including liq. electrolyte engineering, artificial SEI, and solid-state electrolyte technol., to stabilize SEI films are outlined.  Finally, key aspects and prospects in the development of SEI for Na metal anodes are highlighted.  It is believed that this review will serve to further advance the understanding and development of SEIs for Na metal anodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpSg8USrG6SKbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtvgTIuO_vIA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhvVKgtrrL&amp;md5=c4bb91a70c2f4a47c684043b611bb321</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1002/adma.202106565"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ni, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">An MXene-Based Metal Anode with Stepped Sodiophilic Gradient Structure Enables a Large Current Density for Rechargeable Na-O<sub>2</sub> Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">An MXene-Based Metal Anode with Stepped Sodiophilic Gradient Structure Enables a Large Current Density for Rechargeable Na-O2 Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Xin; Ni, Youxuan; Li, Yixin; Sun, Haoxiang; Lu, Yong; Li, Haixia; Yan, Zhenhua; Zhang, Kai; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

Designing a 3D confinement scaffold is a promising strategy for constructing dendrite-free sodium-metal anodes; however, cycling stability at a large c.d. (&gt;10 mA cm-2) is still difficult to realize.  Herein, the design of new lightwt. and fibrous hydroxylated Ti3C2 (h-Ti3C2) MXene based scaffolds with stepped sodiophilic gradient structure (h-M-SSG) is reported, and its thickness can be controlled (80-250μm).  The sodiophilic gradient structure (adjusted by h-Ti3C2) can effectively induce sodium ions to preferentially deposit at the bottom of the scaffold, thus inhibiting dendrite growth. h-M-SSG/Na-based sym. batteries exhibit a low polarization voltage and long cycling life at a high c.d. (40 mA cm-2) and a high cut-off capacity (40 mAh cm-2).  Moreover, a Na-O2 battery with an h-M-SSG/Na anode exhibits a low potential gap of 0.137 V after 45 cycles at 1000 mA g-1 and 1000 mAh g-1.  This deposition-regulation strategy would inspire the design of 3D scaffolds for high-performance sodium-metal-anode-based batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp1k9K2SYYpV7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhtvgTIuO_vIA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB38XlvVeitr0%253D&amp;md5=8c75b8122b31a6c4e1d16630d2b6e4ce</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1039/D1TA00066G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Omar, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oswald, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hantusch, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Giebeler, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nielsch, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mikhailova, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Facile Method to Stabilize Sodium Metal Anodes Towards High-Performance Sodium Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A facile method to stabilize sodium metal anodes towards high-performance sodium batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lu, Qiongqiong; Omar, Ahmad; Ding, Ling; Oswald, Steffen; Hantusch, Martin; Giebeler, Lars; Nielsch, Kornelius; Mikhailova, Daria</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9038-9047</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium metal is the ultimate anode for next generation high-energy-d. sodium metal batteries due to its superior theor. specific capacity, low redox potential, and natural abundance.  However, sodium metal suffers from extreme and uncontrollable dendrite growth and gas evolution problems.  These incidents result in a low coulombic efficiency and safety issues such as dangerous short circuits.  Herein, an effective protective layer is fabricated on the Na metal anode via an extremely facile pretreatment method with 1,3-dioxolane.  The protective layer exhibits fast interfacial transport and a lower resistance.  Direct optical visualization shows that dendrite growth and gas evolution are suppressed due to the introduction of the protective layer.  As a result, an outstanding cycling stability for 2800 h (1400 cycles) at 1 mA cm-2 in a sym. cell is obtained.  Moreover, the full cell using the protected Na metal anode shows superior electrochem. performance in comparison to the untreated Na metal anode.  Furthermore, large format protected Na metal anodes fabricated by spraying 1,3-dioxolane were demonstrated and successfully assembled in pouch cells, showing a stable specific capacity of around 95 mA h g-1.  Thus, our work presents a facile, efficient and scalable protection strategy to stabilize Na metal anodes towards high-energy-d. sodium batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr2OO-XQuc4QrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljBLrVH0l6Pvw"> &gt;&gt; In this Review, the electrochem. mechanism is discussed, thus revealing the main challenges in Na-Se batteries.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Molecular insights into genome-wide association studies of chronic kidney disease-defining traits</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Xiaoguang; Eales, James M.; Akbarov, Artur; Guo, Hui; Becker, Lorenz; Talavera, David; Ashraf, Fehzan; Nawaz, Jabran; Pramanik, Sanjeev; Bowes, John; Jiang, Xiao; Dormer, John; Denniff, Matthew; Antczak, Andrzej; Szulinska, Monika; Wise, Ingrid; Prestes, Priscilla R.; Glyda, Maciej; Bogdanski, Pawel; Zukowska-Szczechowska, Ewa; Berzuini, Carlo; Woolf, Adrian S.; Samani, Nilesh J.; Charchar, Fadi J.; Tomaszewski, Maciej</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

                        cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stable Dendrite-Free Sodium-Sulfur Batteries Enabled by a Localized High-Concentration Electrolyte</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Jiarui; Bhargav, Amruth; Shin, Woochul; Manthiram, Arumugam</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">143</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">48</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ambient-temp. sodium-sulfur batteries are an appealing, sustainable, and low-cost alternative to lithium-ion batteries due to their high material abundance and specific energy of 1274 W h kg-1.  However, their viability is hampered by Na polysulfide (NaPS) shuttling, Na loss due to side reactions with the electrolyte, and dendrite formation.  Here, we demonstrate that a solid-electrolyte interphase rich in inorg. components can be realized at both the sulfur cathode and the Na anode by tweaking the solvation structure of the electrolyte.  This transforms the sulfur redox process from conventional dissoln.-pptn. chem. into a quasi-solid-state reaction, which eliminates NaPS shuttling and facilitates dendrite-free Na-metal plating and stripping.  With the solvated ionic liq. electrolyte structure, a high initial capacity of 922 mA h g-1 with a capacity fade of as low as 0.10% per cycle over 300 cycles was achieved.  The scalability of this approach to pouch cells with practically necessary parameters demonstrates its potential for practical viability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrC-K_BJxx2D7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhzhWTuz7_Zhw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXisFCntL3O&amp;md5=372b63babffe9f9a49b0df95aa996526</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1038/s41563-020-0673-0"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rakov, D.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Engineering high-energy-density sodium battery anodes for improved cycling with superconcentrated ionic-liquid electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Rakov, Dmitrii A.; Chen, Fangfang; Ferdousi, Shammi A.; Li, Hua; Pathirana, Thushan; Simonov, Alexandr N.; Howlett, Patrick C.; Atkin, Rob; Forsyth, Maria</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Abstr.: Non-uniform metal deposition and dendrite formation in high-d. energy storage devices reduces the efficiency, safety and life of batteries with metal anodes.  Superconcd. ionic-liq. electrolytes (for example 1:1 ionic liq.:alkali ion) coupled with anode preconditioning at more neg. potentials can completely mitigate these issues, and therefore revolutionize high-d. energy storage devices.  However, the mechanisms by which very high salt concn. and preconditioning potential enable uniform metal deposition and prevent dendrite formation at the metal anode during cycling are poorly understood, and therefore not optimized.  Here, we use at. force microscopy and mol. dynamics simulations to unravel the influence of these factors on the interface chem. in a sodium electrolyte, demonstrating how a molten-salt-like structure at the electrode surface results in dendrite-free metal cycling at higher rates.  Such a structure will support the formation of a more favorable solid electrolyte interphase, accepted as being a crit. factor in stable battery cycling.  This new understanding will enable engineering of efficient anode electrodes by tuning the interfacial nanostructure via salt concn. and high-voltage preconditioning.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoq4YTBh2XJnrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhzhWTuz7_Zhw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXosVyrtbc%253D&amp;md5=d1c57e17af81eb1c773e3d46260cd837</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201902899"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bay, M. F.; Sakamoto, Jeff; Battaglia, Corsin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">All-solid-state batteries with an alkali metal anode have the potential to achieve high energy d.  However, the onset of dendrite formation limits the max. plating c.d. across the solid electrolyte and prevents fast charging.  It is shown that the max. plating c.d. is related to the interfacial resistance between the solid electrolyte and the metal anode.  Due to their high ionic cond., low electronic cond., and stability against sodium metal, Na-β''-alumina ceramics are excellent candidates as electrolytes for room-temp. all-solid-state batteries.  Here, it is demonstrated that a heat treatment of Na-β''-alumina ceramics in argon atm. enables an interfacial resistance &lt;10 O cm2 and current densities up to 12 mA cm-2 at room temp.  The c.d. obtained for Na-β''-alumina is ten times higher than that measured on a garnet-type Li7La3Zr2O12 electrolyte under equiv. conditions.  XPS shows that eliminating hydroxyl groups and carbon contaminations at the interface between Na-β''-alumina and sodium metal is key to reach such values.  By comparing the temp.-dependent stripping/plating behavior of Na-β''-alumina and Li7La3Zr2O12, the role of the alkali metal in governing interface kinetics is discussed.  This study provides new insights into dendrite formation and paves the way for fast-charging all-solid-state batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpZNzI9TcVUSLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhzhWTuz7_Zhw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXit1Ort77N&amp;md5=aa3a405bdb26e3b5fa7ab25a04842bc0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-017-00742-x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Choudhury, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ozhabes, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gunceler, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zachman, M.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Designing solid-liquid interphases for sodium batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Choudhury Snehashis; Wei Shuya; Shin Jung Hwan; Nath Pooja; Agrawal Akanksha; Archer Lynden A; Ozhabes Yalcin; Gunceler Deniz; Arias Tomas A; Zachman Michael J; Kourkoutis Lena F; Tu Zhengyuan; Kourkoutis Lena F</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Secondary batteries based on earth-abundant sodium metal anodes are desirable for both stationary and portable electrical energy storage.  Room-temperature sodium metal batteries are impractical today because morphological instability during recharge drives rough, dendritic electrodeposition.  Chemical instability of liquid electrolytes also leads to premature cell failure as a result of parasitic reactions with the anode.  Here we use joint density-functional theoretical analysis to show that the surface diffusion barrier for sodium ion transport is a sensitive function of the chemistry of solid-electrolyte interphase.  In particular, we find that a sodium bromide interphase presents an exceptionally low energy barrier to ion transport, comparable to that of metallic magnesium.  We evaluate this prediction by means of electrochemical measurements and direct visualization studies.  These experiments reveal an approximately three-fold reduction in activation energy for ion transport at a sodium bromide interphase.  Direct visualization of sodium electrodeposition confirms large improvements in stability of sodium deposition at sodium bromide-rich interphases.The chemistry at the interface between electrolyte and electrode plays a critical role in determining battery performance.  Here, the authors show that a NaBr enriched solid-electrolyte interphase can lower the surface diffusion barrier for sodium ions, enabling stable electrodeposition.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcSVxZ2bIR2iP6_dlEzl_fBafW6udTcc2eZgjlRnVw3qWrntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1M7gtFSitg%253D%253D&amp;md5=bfba0a07615db3bc176a844fe2bc4835</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1021/acscentsci.5b00328"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Seh, Z.                         cas_cit22R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">22</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Highly Reversible Room-Temperature Sodium Metal Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Seh, Zhi Wei; Sun, Jie; Sun, Yongming; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Central Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">449-455</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACSCII</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2374-7951</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to its low cost and high natural abundance, sodium metal is among the most promising anode materials for energy storage technologies beyond lithium ion batteries.  However, room-temp. sodium metal anodes suffer from poor reversibility during long-term plating and stripping, mainly due to formation of nonuniform solid electrolyte interphase as well as dendritic growth of sodium metal.  Herein it is reported for the first time that a simple liq. electrolyte, sodium hexafluorophosphate in glymes (mono-, di-, and tetraglyme), can enable highly reversible and nondendritic plating-stripping of sodium metal anodes at room temp.  High av. Coulombic efficiencies of 99.9% were achieved over 300 plating-stripping cycles at 0.5 mA cm-2.  The long-term reversibility was found to arise from the formation of a uniform, inorg. solid electrolyte interphase made of sodium oxide and sodium fluoride, which is highly impermeable to electrolyte solvent and conducive to nondendritic growth.  As a proof of concept, a room-temp. sodium-sulfur battery is demonstrated using this class of electrolytes, paving the way for the development of next-generation, sodium-based energy storage technologies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrdqXOWzEW74bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhzhWTuz7_Zhw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhvVGmtrfL&amp;md5=6155e3c781a24b8340081c2ad4444616</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit23" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201606663"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goncharova, L.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Critical Role of Ultrathin Graphene Films with Tunable Thickness in Enabling Highly Stable Sodium Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Huan; Wang, Chuanlong; Matios, Edward; Li, Weiyang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">17</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6808-6815</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na metal showed great promise as an anode material for the next-generation energy storage systems because of its high theor. capacity, low cost, and high earth abundance.  However, the extremely high reactivity of Na metal with org. electrolyte gives unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) and growth of Na dendrites upon repeated electrochem. stripping/plating, causing poor cycling performance, and serious safety issues.  Herein, the authors present highly stable and dendrite-free Na metal anodes over a wide current range and long-term cycling via directly applying free-standing graphene films with tunable thickness on Na metal surface.  The authors systematically study the dependence of Na anode stability on the thickness of the graphene film at different current densities and capacities.  Only a few nanometer (∼2-3 nm) differences in the graphene thickness can have decisive influence on the stability and rate capability of Na anodes.  To achieve the optimal performance, the thickness of the graphene film covered on Na surface needs to be meticulously selected based on the applied c.d.  With a multilayer graphene film (∼5 nm in thickness) as a protective layer, stable Na cycling behavior was 1st achieved in carbonate electrolyte without any additives over 100 cycles at a c.d. ≤2 mA/cm2 with a high capacity of 3 mA h/cm2.  The authors believe the work could be a viable route toward high-energy Na battery systems, and can provide valuable insights into the Li batteries as well.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoKQT_0ZsM4qrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljCusCzV6CiqQ"> &gt;&gt;
